class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;">Many of the 27.7 million Minnan speakers in mainland China (2018), 13.5 million in Taiwan (2017), 2.02 million in Malaysia (2000),<sup class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue_tree-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> 1 million in Philippines (2010), 766,000 in Indonesia (2015), 350,000 in Cambodia (2001), 70,500 in Hong Kong (2016), 45,000 in Vietnam (1989 ), 17,600 in Thailand (1984), 13,300 in Brunei (2004)<sup id="cite_ref-Ethnologue23&#124;nan_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue23|nan-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><div style="display: inline-block; </td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718"><table class="infobox-subbox collapsible collapsed" style="display:inline-table; text-align: left;"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-above" style="font-size: 100%; text-align: left; background-color: #f9ffbc;">Transcriptions</th></tr><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-header" style="background-color: #dcffc9;"><a href="/wiki/Southern_Min" title="Southern Min">Southern Min</a></th></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="font-weight:normal;"><a class="mw-selflink selflink">Hokkien</a> <a href="/wiki/Pe%CC%8Dh-%C5%8De-j%C4%AB" title="Pe̍h-ōe-jī">POJ</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Min Nan Chinese-language romanization"><span style="font-style: normal" lang="nan-Latn">Lán-lâng-ōe / Lán-nâng-ōe / Nán-nâng-ōe</span></span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_Romanization_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Taiwanese Romanization System">Tâi-lô</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Min Nan Chinese-language romanization"><span style="font-style: normal" lang="nan-Latn">Lán-lâng-uē / Lán-nâng-uē / Nán-nâng-uē</span></span></td></tr></tbody></table></td><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718"></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Hokkien</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;h&#39; in &#39;hi&#39;">h</span><span title="/ɒ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;body&#39;">ɒ</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="/i/: &#39;y&#39; in &#39;happy&#39;">i</span><span title="/ɛ/: &#39;e&#39; in &#39;dress&#39;">ɛ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">HOK</span>-ee-en</i></a>)<sup id="cite_ref-OED_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OED-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">variety</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Min" title="Southern Min">Southern Min</a> Chinese languages, native to and originating from the <a href="/wiki/Minnan_region" title="Minnan region">Minnan region</a>, in the southeastern part of <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a> in southeastern <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland China</a>. It is also referred to as <b>Quanzhang</b> (Chinese&#58; <span lang="zh">泉漳</span>; pinyin&#58; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">Quánzhāng</span></i>), from the first characters of the urban centers of <a href="/wiki/Quanzhou" title="Quanzhou">Quanzhou</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zhangzhou" title="Zhangzhou">Zhangzhou</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_Hokkien" title="Taiwanese Hokkien">Taiwanese Hokkien</a> is one of the <a href="/wiki/National_language" title="National language">national languages</a> in <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>. Hokkien is also widely spoken within the overseas <a href="/wiki/Chinese_diaspora" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese diaspora">Chinese diaspora</a> in <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brunei" title="Brunei">Brunei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> elsewhere across the world. The mutual intelligibility between Hokkien dialects varies, but they are still held together by ethnolinguistic identity.<sup id="cite_ref-Minnan_8-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Minnan-8">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Southeast_Asia" title="Maritime Southeast Asia">maritime Southeast Asia</a>, Hokkien historically served as the <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> amongst overseas Chinese communities of <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese_subgroups" title="Han Chinese subgroups">all dialects and subgroups</a>, and it remains today as the most spoken <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">variety of Chinese</a> in the region, including in <a href="/wiki/Singaporean_Hokkien" title="Singaporean Hokkien">Singapore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Penang_Hokkien" title="Penang Hokkien">Malaysia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Hokkien" title="Philippine Hokkien">Philippines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medan_Hokkien" title="Medan Hokkien">Indonesia</a>. This applied to a lesser extent to <a href="/wiki/Mainland_Southeast_Asia" title="Mainland Southeast Asia">mainland Southeast Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Betawi_language" title="Betawi language">Betawi Malay language</a>, spoken by some five million people in and around the Indonesian capital <a href="/wiki/Jakarta" title="Jakarta">Jakarta</a>, includes numerous Hokkien loanwords due to the significant influence of the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Indonesians" title="Chinese Indonesians">Chinese Indonesian</a> diaspora, most of whom are of Hokkien ancestry and origin. <a href="/wiki/Hokkien_Kelantan" title="Hokkien Kelantan">Hokkien Kelantan</a> in northern <a href="/wiki/Peninsular_Malaysia" title="Peninsular Malaysia">Malaya</a> of <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hokaglish" title="Hokaglish">Hokaglish</a> spoken sporadically across the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Metro_Manila" title="Metro Manila">Metro Manila</a> are also <a href="/wiki/Mixed_language" title="Mixed language">mixed languages</a> with Hokkien as the base <a href="/wiki/Lexifier" title="Lexifier">lexifier</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Names"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Various populations of Hokkien speakers refer to the language as:
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Min Nan Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="nan-Latn">Bân-lâm-gú / Bân-lâm-gí / Bân-lâm-gír / Bân-lâm-ú</i></span> (<span lang="zh-Hans">闽南语</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">閩南語</span>, literally 'Southern Min language') in China, Taiwan,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> and Malaysia</li>
<li><span title="Min Nan Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="nan-Latn">Bân-lâm-ōe / Bân-lâm-ōa / Bîn-lâm-ōe</i></span> (<span lang="zh-Hans">闽南话</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">閩南話</span>, literally 'Southern Min language') in China, Taiwan, Philippines, Malaysia</li>
<li><span title="Min Nan Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="nan-Latn">Tâi-gí / Tâi-gú</i></span> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant-TW">臺語</span></span>, literally 'Taiwanese language') or <span title="Min Nan Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="nan-Latn">Ho̍h-ló-ōe / Hô-ló-ōe</i></span> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant-TW">福佬話</span></span>, literally 'Hoklo Speech') in Taiwan</li>
<li><span title="Min Nan Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="nan-Latn">Lán-lâng-ōe / Lán-nâng-ōe / Nán-nâng-ōe</i></span> (<span title="Min Nan Chinese-language text"><span lang="nan-PH">咱人話／咱儂話</span></span>, literally 'Our people's language') in the Philippines</li>
<li><span title="Min Nan Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="nan-Latn">Hok-kiàn-ōe</i></span> / <span title="Min Nan Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="nan-Latn">Hok-kiàn-ōa</i></span> (<span lang="zh">福建話</span>, literally 'Hokkien language') in Malaysia, Singapore, Indonesia, Brunei, Philippines</li></ul>
<p>In parts of Southeast Asia and in the English-speaking communities, the term <i>Hokkien</i> (<span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[hɔk˥kiɛn˨˩]</span>) is etymologically derived from the Southern Min pronunciation for Fujian (<span lang="zh">福建</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">Fújiàn</span></i>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">Hok-kiàn</span></i>), the province from which the language hails. In Southeast Asia and the English press, <i>Hokkien</i> is used in common parlance to refer to the Southern Min dialects of southern Fujian, and does not include reference to dialects of other Sinitic branches also present in Fujian such as the <a href="/wiki/Fuzhou_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Fuzhou language">Fuzhou language</a> (<a href="/wiki/Eastern_Min" title="Eastern Min">Eastern Min</a>), <a href="/wiki/Pu-Xian_Min" class="mw-redirect" title="Pu-Xian Min">Pu-Xian Min</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Min" title="Northern Min">Northern Min</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gan_Chinese" title="Gan Chinese">Gan Chinese</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hakka_Chinese" title="Hakka Chinese">Hakka</a>.
</p><p>The word <i>Hokkien</i> first originated from <a href="/wiki/Walter_Henry_Medhurst" title="Walter Henry Medhurst">Walter Henry Medhurst</a> when he published the <i>Dictionary of the Hok-këèn Dialect of the Chinese Language, According to the Reading and Colloquial Idioms</i> in 1832. This is considered to be the earliest English-based Hokkien Dictionary and the first major reference work in POJ, although the romanization within was quite different from the modern system. In this dictionary, the word "Hok-këèn" was used. In 1869, POJ was further revised by John Macgowan in his published book <i>A Manual Of The Amoy Colloquial</i>. In this book, "këèn" was changed to "kien" as "Hok-kien" and from then on, the word "Hokkien" began to be used more often.
</p><p>Historically, Hokkien was also known as <a href="/wiki/Amoy_dialect" title="Amoy dialect"><i>Amoy</i></a>, after the Hokkien name for <a href="/wiki/Xiamen" title="Xiamen">Xiamen</a>, the principal port in southern Fujian during the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a>, as one of the five ports opened to foreign trade by the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Nanking" title="Treaty of Nanking">Treaty of Nanking</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> By 1873, Rev. <a href="/wiki/Carstairs_Douglas" title="Carstairs Douglas">Carstairs Douglas</a> would publish his "<i>Chinese–English Dictionary of the Vernacular or Spoken Language of Amoy, With the Principal Variations of the Chang-chew and Chin-chew Dialects."</i> where he would call the language the "Language of Amoy"<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> or "<i>The Amoy Vernacular</i>"<sup id="cite_ref-:3_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and by 1883, Rev. John Macgowan would publish another dictionary, the "<i>English and Chinese Dictionary of the Amoy Dialect</i>".<sup id="cite_ref-:4_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Due to confusion with differentiating the Amoy dialect of Hokkien from Xiamen with the general language itself, many proscribe this usage though many old books and media may still be observed to be labeled with "Amoy" to refer to the language in general, besides the specific dialect of Hokkien from Xiamen.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">Geographic distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Hokkien is spoken in the southern seaward quarter of Fujian, southeastern <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a>, as well as the eastern part of <a href="/wiki/Namoa" class="mw-redirect" title="Namoa">Namoa</a> in China; Taiwan; <a href="/wiki/Metro_Manila" title="Metro Manila">Metro Manila</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metro_Cebu" title="Metro Cebu">Metro Cebu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metro_Davao" title="Metro Davao">Metro Davao</a> and other cities in the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>; Singapore; Brunei; <a href="/wiki/Medan" title="Medan">Medan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Riau" title="Riau">Riau</a> and other cities in <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>; and from <a href="/wiki/Perlis" title="Perlis">Perlis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kedah" title="Kedah">Kedah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Penang" title="Penang">Penang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Klang_(city)" title="Klang (city)">Klang</a> in Malaysia.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox-move{border-left:10px solid #9932cc}.mw-parser-output .ambox-protection{border-left:10px solid #a2a9b1}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-text{border:none;padding:0.25em .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">November 2018</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Hokkien originated in the southern area of Fujian province, an important center for trade and migration, and has since become one of the most common Chinese varieties overseas. The major pole of Hokkien varieties outside of Fujian is nearby Taiwan, where immigrants from Fujian arrived as workers during the 40 years of <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Formosa" title="Dutch Formosa">Dutch rule</a>, fleeing the Qing dynasty during the 20 years of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Tungning" title="Kingdom of Tungning">Ming loyalist rule</a>, as immigrants during the 200 years of <a href="/wiki/Taiwan_under_Qing_rule" title="Taiwan under Qing rule">Qing dynasty rule</a>, especially in the last 120 years after immigration restrictions were relaxed, and even as immigrants during the period of <a href="/wiki/Taiwan_under_Japanese_rule" title="Taiwan under Japanese rule">Japanese rule</a>. The Taiwanese dialect mostly has origins with the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tong%27an_Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Tong&#39;an Hokkien (page does not exist)">Tung'an</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quanzhou_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Quanzhou dialect">Quanzhou</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zhangzhou_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Zhangzhou dialect">Zhangzhou</a> variants, but since then, the Amoy dialect, also known as the Xiamen dialect, has become the modern <a href="/wiki/Prestige_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Prestige dialect">prestige representative</a> for the language in China. Both <i>Amoy</i> and <i>Xiamen</i> come from the Chinese name of the city (Chinese&#58; <span lang="zh-Hans">厦门</span>; pinyin&#58; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">Xiàmén</span></i>; Pe̍h-ōe-jī&#58; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">Ē-mûi</span></i>); the former is from Zhangzhou Hokkien, whereas the latter comes from Mandarin.
</p><p>There are many Min Nan speakers among <a href="/wiki/Overseas_Chinese" title="Overseas Chinese">overseas Chinese</a> communities in Southeast Asia, as well as in the United States (<a href="/wiki/Hoklo_Americans" title="Hoklo Americans">Hoklo Americans</a>). Many ethnic <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> emigrants to the region were <a href="/wiki/Hoklo_people" title="Hoklo people">Hoklo</a> from southern Fujian, and brought the language to what is now Myanmar, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, Indonesia (the former <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_Indies" title="Dutch East Indies">Dutch East Indies</a>) and present day Malaysia and Singapore (formerly <a href="/wiki/British_Malaya" title="British Malaya">Malaya</a> and the <a href="/wiki/British_Straits_Settlements" class="mw-redirect" title="British Straits Settlements">British Straits Settlements</a>). Most of the Min Nan dialects of this region have incorporated some foreign loanwords. Hokkien is reportedly the native language of up to 80% of the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Filipino" title="Chinese Filipino">ethnic Chinese people</a> in the Philippines, among which is known locally as <i><a href="/wiki/Philippine_Hokkien" title="Philippine Hokkien">Lán-nâng-uē</a></i> ("Our people's speech"). Hokkien speakers form the largest group of overseas Chinese in Singapore, Malaysia, Indonesia and Philippines.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Classification"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485"><table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Hokkien" title="Special:EditPage/Hokkien">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>&#32;in this section. <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r997900035">.mw-parser-output .locmap .od{position:absolute}.mw-parser-output .locmap .id{position:absolute;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .locmap class="locmap noviewer noresize thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px"><div style="position:relative;width:250px;border:1px solid lightgray"><span class="notpageimage" title="Xiamen"><img alt="Xiamen" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Red_pog.svg/8px-Red_pog.svg.png" decoding="async" width="8" height="8" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Red_pog.svg/12px-Red_pog.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Red_pog.svg/16px-Red_pog.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="64" data-file-height="64" /></span></span></div><div class="pr" style="font-size:91%;width:6em;left:5px"><div><a href="/wiki/Xiamen" title="Xiamen">Xiamen</a></div></div></div></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:China_Fujian2_location_map.svg" title="File:China Fujian2 location map.svg">class=notpageimage| </a></div>Locations of Hokkien varieties in Fujian</div></div></div>
<p>Southern Fujian is home to four principal Hokkien dialects: <a href="/wiki/Zhangzhou_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Zhangzhou dialect">Chiangchew</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quanzhou_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Quanzhou dialect">Chinchew</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tong%27an_dialect&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Tong&#39;an dialect (page does not exist)">Tung'an</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Amoy_dialect" title="Amoy dialect">Amoy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Amoy-English_Dictionary1_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amoy-English_Dictionary1-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> originating from the cities of Quanzhou, Zhangzhou, historical <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tung%27an_County&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Tung&#39;an County (page does not exist)">Tung'an County</a> (<span lang="zh">同安縣</span>, now Xiamen and <a href="/wiki/Kinmen" title="Kinmen">Kinmen</a>) and her own <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Xiamen" title="Port of Xiamen">Port of Amoy</a>, respectively.
</p><p>In the late 1800s, the Amoy dialect attracted special attention, because Amoy was one of the five ports opened to foreign trade by the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Nanking" title="Treaty of Nanking">Treaty of Nanking</a>, but before that it had not attracted attention.<sup id="cite_ref-Amoy-English_Dictionary2_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amoy-English_Dictionary2-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The Amoy dialect is adopted as the 'Modern Representative Min Nan'. The Amoy dialect <em>cannot</em> simply be interpreted as a mixture of the Zhangzhou and Quanzhou dialects, but rather it is formed on the foundation of the Tung'an dialect with further inputs from other sub-dialects.<sup id="cite_ref-第十屆閩方言國際學術研討會_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-第十屆閩方言國際學術研討會-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> It has played an influential role in history, especially in the <a href="/wiki/Western_imperialism_in_Asia#Western_European_and_Russian_intrusions_into_China" title="Western imperialism in Asia">relations of Western nations with China</a>, and was one of the most frequently learnt dialect of the Hokkien variety by Westerners during the second half of the 19th century and the early 20th century.
</p><p>The Modern Representative form of Hokkien spoken around the Taiwanese city of <a href="/wiki/Tainan" title="Tainan">Tainan</a> (<span lang="zh">台南</span>) heavily resembles the Tung'an dialect.<sup id="cite_ref-臺南閩南語1_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-臺南閩南語1-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-臺南閩南語2_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-臺南閩南語2-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> All Hokkien dialects spoken throughout the whole of Taiwan are collectively known as Taiwanese Hokkien, or Holo locally, although there is a tendency to call these Taiwanese language for historical reasons. It is spoken by more Taiwanese than any Sinitic language except Mandarin, and it is known by a majority of the population;<sup id="cite_ref-:02_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> thus, from a <a href="/wiki/Socio-political" class="mw-redirect" title="Socio-political">socio-political</a> perspective, it forms a significant pole of language usage due to the popularity of Holo-language media. Douglas (1873/1899) also noted that <i>Formosa</i> (<a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Taiwan" title="Geography of Taiwan">Taiwan</a>) has been settled mainly by emigrants from <i>Amo</i>y (Xiamen), <i>Chang-chew</i> (Zhangzhou), and <i>Chin-chew</i> (Quanzhou). Several parts of the island are usually found to be specially inhabited by descendants of such emigrants, but in Taiwan, the various forms of the dialects mentioned prior are a good deal mixed up.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southeast_Asia">Southeast Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Southeast Asia"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The varieties of Hokkien in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> originate from these dialects. Douglas (1873–1899) notes that
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Singapore and the various <a href="/wiki/Straits_Settlements" title="Straits Settlements">Straits Settlements</a><i> &#91;such as Penang and <a href="/wiki/Malacca" title="Malacca">Malacca</a>&#93;</i>, Batavia<i> &#91;Jakarta&#93; </i>and other parts of the Dutch possessions<i> &#91;<a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>&#93;</i>, are crowded with emigrants, especially from the Chang-chew<i> &#91;Zhangzhou&#93; </i>prefecture; Manila and other parts of the Philippines have great numbers from Chin-chew<i> &#91;Quanzhou&#93;</i>, and emigrants are largely scattered in like manner in Siam<i> &#91;<a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>&#93;</i>, Burmah<i> &#91;<a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>&#93;</i>, the Malay Peninsula<i> &#91;peninsular Malaysia&#93;</i>, Cochin China<i> &#91;<a href="/wiki/Southern_Vietnam" title="Southern Vietnam">Southern Vietnam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a>&#93;</i>, Saigon<i> &#91;<a href="/wiki/Ho_Chi_Minh_City" title="Ho Chi Minh City">Ho Chi Minh City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>&#93;</i>, &amp;c. In many of these places there is also a great mixture of emigrants from Swatow<i> &#91;<a href="/wiki/Shantou" title="Shantou">Shantou</a>&#93;</i>.<i>"<sup id="cite_ref-:2_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></i></p></blockquote>
<p>In modern times though, a mixed dialect descended from the <a href="/wiki/Quanzhou_dialects" title="Quanzhou dialects">Quanzhou</a>, Amoy, and <a href="/wiki/Zhangzhou_dialects" title="Zhangzhou dialects">Zhangzhou dialects</a>, leaning a little closer to the Quanzhou dialect, possibly due to being from the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tong%27an_Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Tong&#39;an Hokkien (page does not exist)">Tung'an</a> dialect, is spoken by <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Singaporean" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese Singaporean">Chinese Singaporeans</a>, Southern <a href="/wiki/Malaysian_Chinese" title="Malaysian Chinese">Malaysian Chinese</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Indonesian" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese Indonesian">Chinese Indonesians</a> in Indonesia's <a href="/wiki/Riau" title="Riau">Riau</a> province and <a href="/wiki/Riau_Islands" title="Riau Islands">Riau Islands</a>. Variants include <a href="/wiki/Southern_Peninsular_Malaysian_Hokkien" title="Southern Peninsular Malaysian Hokkien">Southern Peninsular Malaysian Hokkien</a> and <a href="/wiki/Singaporean_Hokkien" title="Singaporean Hokkien">Singaporean Hokkien</a> in Singapore.
</p><p>Among Malaysian Chinese of Penang, and other states in northern mainland Malaysia and ethnic Chinese Indonesians in Medan, with other areas in <a href="/wiki/North_Sumatra" title="North Sumatra">North Sumatra</a>, Indonesia, a distinct descendant dialect form of <a href="/wiki/Zhangzhou_dialects" title="Zhangzhou dialects">Zhangzhou Hokkien</a> has developed. In <a href="/wiki/Penang" title="Penang">Penang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kedah" title="Kedah">Kedah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perlis" title="Perlis">Perlis</a>, it is called <a href="/wiki/Penang_Hokkien" title="Penang Hokkien">Penang Hokkien</a> while across the <a href="/wiki/Malacca_Strait" class="mw-redirect" title="Malacca Strait">Malacca Strait</a> in <a href="/wiki/Medan" title="Medan">Medan</a>, an almost identical variant is known as <a href="/wiki/Medan_Hokkien" title="Medan Hokkien">Medan Hokkien</a>.
</p><p>As for <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Filipino" title="Chinese Filipino">Chinese Filipinos</a> in the Philippines, a variant known as <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Hokkien" title="Philippine Hokkien">Philippine Hokkien</a>, which is also mostly derived from Quanzhou Hokkien, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Jinjiang,_Fujian" class="mw-redirect" title="Jinjiang, Fujian">Jinjiang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nan%27an,_Fujian" title="Nan&#39;an, Fujian">Nan'an</a> dialects with a bit of influence from the Amoy (Xiamen) dialect, is still spoken amongst families as most also profess ancestors from the aforementioned areas.
</p><p>There are also Hokkien speakers scattered throughout other parts of Indonesia (such as Jakarta and around the island of <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>), Thailand (especially <a href="/wiki/Southern_Thailand" title="Southern Thailand">Southern Thailand</a> on the border with Malaysia), <a href="/wiki/Chinese_people_in_Myanmar" title="Chinese people in Myanmar">Myanmar</a>, other parts of Malaysia (such as <a href="/wiki/East_Malaysia" title="East Malaysia">insular Malaysia</a>), Brunei, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Cambodians" title="Chinese Cambodians">Cambodia</a>, and Southern Vietnam (such as in Ho Chi Minh City), though there are notably more of <a href="/wiki/Teochew_dialect" title="Teochew dialect">Teochew</a>/<a href="/wiki/Swatow_dialect" title="Swatow dialect">Swatow</a> background among descendants of Chinese migrants in regions such as parts of Peninsular Malaysia, Thailand, Cambodia, Laos, and Southern Vietnam.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485"><table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Hokkien" title="Special:EditPage/Hokkien">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>&#32;in this section. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">January 2016</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Variants of Hokkien dialects can be traced to 2-4 main principal dialects of origin: the original two being, the Quanzhou (<span lang="zh-Hant">泉州</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">Choân-chiu / Chôaⁿ-chiu</span></i>) and Zhangzhou (<span lang="zh-Hant">漳州</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">Chiang-chiu / Cheng-chiu</span></i>) dialects, and in later centuries Xiamen/Amoy (<span lang="zh-Hant">廈門</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">Ē-mn̂g / Ēe-mûi</span></i>) and <a href="/wiki/Tong%27an_District" class="mw-redirect" title="Tong&#39;an District">Tong'an</a> (<span lang="zh-Hant">同安</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">Tâng-oaⁿ</span></i>) as well. The Amoy and Tong'an dialects are historically mixtures of Quanzhou and <a href="/wiki/Zhangzhou_dialects" title="Zhangzhou dialects">Zhangzhou dialects</a>, since they are the geographic and linguistic midpoint between the two, while the rest of the Hokkien dialects spoken in Taiwan and Southeast Asia are respectively derived from varying proportions of the above principal dialects in southern Fujian.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southern_Fujian">Southern Fujian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Southern Fujian"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms" title="Three Kingdoms">Three Kingdoms</a> period of ancient China, there was constant warfare occurring in the <a href="/wiki/Zhongyuan" title="Zhongyuan">Central Plains</a> of China. Ethnic <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> gradually migrated from <a href="/wiki/Henan" title="Henan">Henan</a> to the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Yangtze" title="Yangtze">Yangtze</a> to the coasts of <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a> and later began to enter into the <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a> region, which in ancient times was originally <a href="/wiki/Minyue" title="Minyue">Minyue</a> (<span lang="zh-Hant">閩越</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">Bân Oa̍t</span></i> country, populated with non-Chinese <a href="/wiki/Baiyue" title="Baiyue">Minyue peoples</a>, causing the region for the first time in ancient times to incorporate Old Chinese dialects of which would later become <a href="/wiki/Min_Chinese" title="Min Chinese">Min Chinese</a>. The massive migration of Han Chinese into Fujian region mainly occurred after the <a href="/wiki/Disaster_of_Yongjia" title="Disaster of Yongjia">Disaster of Yongjia</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Jin_Dynasty_(265%E2%80%93420)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jin Dynasty (265–420)">Jìn</a> court fled from the north to the south, causing large numbers of Han Chinese to move into Fujian region. They brought the <a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">Old Chinese</a> spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Central_Plain_(China)" class="mw-redirect" title="Central Plain (China)">Central Plain</a> of China from the prehistoric era to the 3rd century into Fujian that later became Min, which later split off into its respective branches, of which Hokkien descends from the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Min" title="Southern Min">Southern Min</a> branch of it.
</p><p>In 677 (during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Gaozong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Gaozong of Tang">Emperor Gaozong of Tang</a>), <a href="/wiki/Chen_Zheng_(Tang_Dynasty)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chen Zheng (Tang Dynasty)">Chen Zheng</a>, together with his son <a href="/wiki/Chen_Yuanguang" class="mw-redirect" title="Chen Yuanguang">Chen Yuanguang</a>, led a military expedition to suppress a rebellion of the <a href="/wiki/She_people" title="She people">She people</a>. In 885, (during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Xizong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Xizong of Tang">Emperor Xizong of Tang</a>), the two brothers <a href="/wiki/Wang_Chao_(Tang_Dynasty)" class="mw-redirect" title="Wang Chao (Tang Dynasty)">Wang Chao</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wang_Shenzhi" title="Wang Shenzhi">Wang Shenzhi</a>, led a military expedition force to suppress the <a href="/wiki/Huang_Chao" title="Huang Chao">Huang Chao</a> rebellion.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Waves of migration from the north in this era brought the language of <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a> into the Fujian region, which gave Hokkien and all the other Min languages its <a href="/wiki/Literary_and_colloquial_readings_of_Chinese_characters" title="Literary and colloquial readings of Chinese characters">literary readings</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Xiamen_.28Amoy.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Xiamen_(Amoy)">Xiamen (Amoy)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Xiamen (Amoy)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During around the late 17th century when <a href="/wiki/Haijin" title="Haijin">sea bans</a> were lifted, the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Xiamen" title="Port of Xiamen">Port of Xiamen</a> (Amoy), which overshadowed the old port of <a href="/wiki/Yuegang" title="Yuegang">Yuegang</a>, became Fujian's main port where trade was legalized. From then, the Amoy dialect became the main principal dialect spoken overseas, such as in <a href="/wiki/Taiwan_under_Qing_rule" title="Taiwan under Qing rule">Qing-ruled Taiwan</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Malaya" title="British Malaya">British Malaya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Straits_Settlements" title="Straits Settlements">British Straits Settlements</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Singapore_in_the_Straits_Settlements" title="Singapore in the Straits Settlements">British Singapore</a>), <a href="/wiki/British_Hong_Kong" title="British Hong Kong">British Hong Kong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Captaincy_General_of_the_Philippines" title="Captaincy General of the Philippines">Spanish Philippines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insular_Government_of_the_Philippine_Islands" title="Insular Government of the Philippine Islands">American Philippines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_Indies" title="Dutch East Indies">Dutch East Indies</a> (Indonesia), <a href="/wiki/French_Cochinchina" title="French Cochinchina">French Cochinchina</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Ho_Chi_Minh_City" title="Ho Chi Minh City">Saigon</a>), etc. Historically, Port of Xiamen had always been part of Tung'an country until after 1912 of <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_(1912%E2%80%931949)" title="Republic of China (1912–1949)">Republic of China</a> era.<sup id="cite_ref-第十屆閩方言國際學術研討會_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-第十屆閩方言國際學術研討會-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The Amoy dialect was the main prestige form of Hokkien that was mainly known from the late 17th century to around the Republic of China era and varyingly dated these days. Due to that, dictionaries and bibles and other books about Hokkien from recent centuries and even to this day in certain places, like schools and churches, of certain countries, the Hokkien language is still known as <i>"Amoy"</i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_sources">Early sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Early sources"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several playscripts survive from the late 16th century, written in a mixture of Quanzhou and Chaozhou dialects. The most important is the <i><a href="/wiki/Romance_of_the_Litchi_Mirror" class="mw-redirect" title="Romance of the Litchi Mirror">Romance of the Litchi Mirror</a></i>, with extant manuscripts dating from 1566 and 1581.<sup id="cite_ref-Chappell_Peyraube_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chappell_Peyraube-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lien_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lien-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 17th century, <a href="/wiki/Friars_in_Spanish_Philippines" title="Friars in Spanish Philippines">Spanish friars in the Philippines</a> produced materials documenting the Hokkien varieties spoken by the Chinese trading community who had settled there in the late 16th century:<sup id="cite_ref-Chappell_Peyraube_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chappell_Peyraube-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i>Doctrina Christiana en letra y lengua china</i> (1593), a Hokkien version of the <i><a href="/wiki/Doctrina_Christiana" title="Doctrina Christiana">Doctrina Christiana</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:Catálogo_BNE_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:Catálogo_BNE-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Dictionarium Sino Hispanicum</i> (1604), by <a href="/wiki/Pedro_Chirino" title="Pedro Chirino">Pedro Chirino</a><sup id="cite_ref-:5_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Vocabulario de la Lengua Española y China / Vocabulario Hispanico y Chinico</i><sup id="cite_ref-:5_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Bocabulario de la lengua sangleya</i> <i>por las letraz de el A.B.C.</i> (1617), a Spanish–Hokkien dictionary, with definitions.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Arte de la Lengua Chiõ Chiu</i> (1620), a Spanish–Hokkien grammar.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Dictionario Hispanico Sinicum</i> (1626–1642), a primarily Spanish-Hokkien dictionary (with additional incomplete <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin</a> part), giving equivalent words, but not definitions.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Vocabulario de letra china</i> (1643), by Francisco Diaz<sup id="cite_ref-:5_34-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>These texts appear to record a primarily <a href="/wiki/Zhangzhou_dialects" title="Zhangzhou dialects">Zhangzhou-descended dialect</a> with some attested Quanzhou and <a href="/wiki/Chaoshan_Min" title="Chaoshan Min">Teo-Swa</a> features, from the old port of <a href="/wiki/Yuegang" title="Yuegang">Yuegang</a> (modern-day <a href="/wiki/Haicheng,_Fujian" title="Haicheng, Fujian">Haicheng</a>, an old port that is now part of <a href="/wiki/Longhai_City" class="mw-redirect" title="Longhai City">Longhai</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century_sources">19th century sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 19th century sources"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Chinese scholars produced <a href="/wiki/Rhyme_dictionary" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhyme dictionary">rhyme dictionaries</a> describing Hokkien varieties at the beginning of the 19th century:<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i><span lang="nan-Latn">Lūi-im Biāu-ngō͘ (Huìyīn Miàowù)</span></i> (<span lang="zh">彙音妙悟</span> "Understanding of the collected sounds") was written around 1800 by Huang Qian (<span lang="zh">黃謙</span>), and describes the Quanzhou dialect. The oldest extant edition dates from 1831.</li>
<li><i><span lang="nan-Latn">Lūi-chi̍p Ngé-sio̍k-thong Si̍p-ngó͘-im (Huìjí Yǎsútōng Shíwǔyīn)</span></i> (<span lang="zh">彙集雅俗通十五音</span> "Compilation of the fifteen elegant and vulgar sounds") by Xie Xiulan (<span lang="zh">謝秀嵐</span>) describes the Zhangzhou dialect. The oldest extant edition dates from 1818.</li></ul>
<p>Rev. <a href="/wiki/Walter_Henry_Medhurst" title="Walter Henry Medhurst">Walter Henry Medhurst</a> based his 1832 dictionary, <i>"A Dictionary of the Hok-këèn Dialect of the Chinese Language"</i>, on the latter work.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other popular 19th century works are also like those of Rev. John Macgowan's 1883 dictionary, <i>"English and Chinese Dictionary of the Amoy Dialect"</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-:4_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> and Rev. <a href="/wiki/Carstairs_Douglas" title="Carstairs Douglas">Carstairs Douglas</a>'s 1873 dictionary, <i>"Chinese-English Dictionary of the Vernacular or Spoken Language of Amoy, with the Principal Variations of the Chang-Chew and Chin-Chew Dialects"</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> and its 1899 New Edition with Rev. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Barclay_(missionary)" title="Thomas Barclay (missionary)">Thomas Barclay</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Amoy-English_Dictionary1_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amoy-English_Dictionary1-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Phonology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Hokkien has one of the most diverse <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">phoneme</a> inventories among Chinese varieties, with more <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">consonants</a> than <a href="/wiki/Standard_Mandarin" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard Mandarin">Standard Mandarin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a>. <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">Vowels</a> are more-or-less similar to that of Mandarin. Hokkien varieties retain many pronunciations that are no longer found in other Chinese varieties. These include the retention of the <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t/</span> initial, which is now <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/tʂ/</span> (pinyin <i><span lang="zh-Latn">zh</span></i>) in Mandarin (e.g. <span lang="zh">竹</span>; &#39;bamboo&#39; is <i><span lang="nan-Latn">tik</span></i>, but <i><span lang="zh-Latn">zhú</span></i> in Mandarin), having disappeared before the 6th century in other Chinese varieties.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Along with other Min languages, which are not directly <a href="/wiki/Daughter_language" title="Daughter language">descended</a> from <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a>, Hokkien is of considerable interest to <a href="/wiki/Historical_linguistics" title="Historical linguistics">historical linguists</a> for <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_reconstruction" title="Linguistic reconstruction">reconstructing</a> Old Chinese.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initials">Initials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Initials"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Southern Min has <a href="/wiki/Aspiration_(phonetics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Aspiration (phonetics)">aspirated</a>, unaspirated as well as <a href="/wiki/Voice_(phonetics)" title="Voice (phonetics)">voiced consonant</a> initials. For example, the word <span lang="zh-Hant">開</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">khui</span></i>; &#39;open&#39; and <span lang="zh-Hant">關</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">kuiⁿ</span></i>; &#39;close&#39; have the same vowel but differ only by aspiration of the initial and <a href="/wiki/Nasalization" title="Nasalization">nasality</a> of the vowel. In addition, Southern Min has <a href="/wiki/Labial_consonant" title="Labial consonant">labial initial consonants</a> such as <i><span lang="nan-Latn">m</span></i> in <span lang="zh">命</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">miā</span></i>; &#39;life&#39;.
</p><p>Another example is <span lang="zh"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%9F%A5%E5%9F%94%E5%9B%9D" class="extiw" title="wikt:查埔囝">查埔囝</a></span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">cha-po͘-kiáⁿ / ta-po͘-kiáⁿ / ta-po͘-káⁿ</span></i>; &#39;boy&#39; and <span lang="zh"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%9F%A5%E6%9F%90%E5%9B%9D" class="extiw" title="wikt:查某囝">查某囝</a></span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">cha-bó͘-kiáⁿ / cha̋u-kiáⁿ / cha̋u-káⁿ / chő͘-kiáⁿ</span></i>; &#39;girl&#39;, which for the <i>cha-po͘-kiáⁿ</i> and <i>cha-bó͘-kiáⁿ</i> pronunciation differ only in the second syllable in consonant voicing and in <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">tone</a>.
</p>

<ul><li>All consonants but <span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">ʔ</a></span> may be nasalized; voiced oral stops may be nasalized into voiced nasal stops.</li>
<li>Nasal stops mostly occur word-initially.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Quanzhou and nearby may pronounce <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">⟨j⟩</span>/<span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">⟨dz⟩</span> as <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">⟨l⟩</span> or <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">⟨g⟩</span>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="gl is dubious (May 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li>
<li><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">⟨l⟩</span> is often interchanged with <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">⟨n⟩</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">⟨j⟩</span>/<span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">⟨dz⟩</span> throughout different dialects.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">⟨j⟩</span>, sometimes into <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">⟨dz⟩</span>, is often pronounced very 'thick' so as to change to <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">⟨l⟩</span>, or very nearly so.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Some dialects may pronounce <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">⟨l⟩</span> as <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">⟨d⟩</span>, or a sound very like it.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Approximant sounds [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_labial%E2%80%93velar_approximant" title="Voiced labial–velar approximant">w</a></span>] [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_palatal_approximant" title="Voiced palatal approximant">j</a></span>], only occur word-medially, and are also realized as laryngealized [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Creaky_voice" title="Creaky voice">w̰</a></span>] [<span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Creaky_voice" title="Creaky voice">j̰</a></span>], within a few medial and terminal environments.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Finals">Finals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Finals"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Unlike Mandarin, Hokkien retains all the final consonants corresponding to those of Middle Chinese. While Mandarin only preserves the <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[n]</span> and  <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ŋ]</span> finals, Southern Min also preserves the  <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[m]</span>, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[p]</span>, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[t]</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[k]</span> finals and has developed the <a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">glottal stop</a> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʔ]</span>.
</p><p>The vowels of Hokkien are listed below:<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<ul><li>Oral vowel sounds are realized as nasal sounds when preceding a nasal consonant.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialectal_sound_shifts">Dialectal sound shifts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Dialectal sound shifts"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The following table illustrates some of the more commonly seen sound shifts between various dialects. Pronunciations are provided in <a href="/wiki/Pe%CC%8Dh-%C5%8De-j%C4%AB" title="Pe̍h-ōe-jī">Pe̍h-ōe-jī</a> and <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">IPA</a>.

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tones">Tones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Tones"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the traditional Chinese system, Hokkien dialects have 7 or 8 distinct tones, including two <a href="/wiki/Entering_tone" class="mw-redirect" title="Entering tone">entering tones</a> which end in <a href="/wiki/Plosive" title="Plosive">plosive</a> consonants. The entering tones can be analysed as <a href="/wiki/Allophone" title="Allophone">allophones</a>, giving 5 or 6 <a href="/wiki/Phonemic" class="mw-redirect" title="Phonemic">phonemic</a> <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">tones</a>. In addition, many dialects have an additional phonemic tone ("tone 9" according to the traditional reckoning), used only in special or foreign loan words.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> This means that Hokkien dialects have between 5 and 7 phonemic tones.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tone_sandhi" title="Tone sandhi">Tone sandhi</a> is extensive.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> There are minor variations between the Quanzhou and Zhangzhou tone systems. Taiwanese tones follow the patterns of Amoy or Quanzhou, depending on the area of Taiwan.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects">Dialects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Dialects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_Hokkien#Quanzhou–Zhangzhou_inclinations" title="Taiwanese Hokkien">Taiwanese Hokkien §&#160;Quanzhou–Zhangzhou inclinations</a></div>
<p>Minnan is spoken in a variety of accents and dialects across the <a href="/wiki/Minnan_region" title="Minnan region">Minnan region</a>. The Hokkien spoken in most areas of the three counties of southern Zhangzhou have merged the coda finals -n and -ng into -ng. The initial consonant j (<a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_affricate" title="Voiced alveolar affricate">dz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolo-palatal_affricate" title="Voiced alveolo-palatal affricate">dʑ</a>) is not present in most dialects of Hokkien spoken in Quanzhou, having been merged into the <a href="/wiki/Voiced_dental_and_alveolar_stops" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiced dental and alveolar stops">d</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dental,_alveolar_and_postalveolar_lateral_approximants" class="mw-redirect" title="Dental, alveolar and postalveolar lateral approximants">l</a> initials.
</p><p>The -ik or -ɪk final consonant that is preserved in the native Hokkien dialects of Zhangzhou and Xiamen is also preserved in the Nan'an dialect (<span lang="zh">色</span>, <span lang="zh">德</span>, <span lang="zh">竹</span>) but are pronounced as -iak in Quanzhou Hokkien.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1116488514">.mw-parser-output .treeview ul{padding:0;margin:0}.mw-parser-output .treeview li{padding:0;margin:0;list-style-type:none;list-style-image:none}.mw-parser-output .treeview li li{background:url("https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f2/Treeview-grey-line.png")no-repeat 0 -2981px;padding-left:21px;text-indent:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .treeview li li:last-child{background-position:0 -5971px}.mw-parser-output .treeview li.emptyline>ul>.mw-empty-elt:first-child+.emptyline,.mw-parser-output .treeview li.emptyline>ul>li:first-child{background-position:0 9px}</style><div class="treeview">
<ul><li><b>Hokkien</b>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Quanzhou_dialects" title="Quanzhou dialects">Quanzhou Hokkien dialects</a> (<span lang="zh">泉州腔</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">Choân-chiu khiuⁿ</span></i>):
<ul><li>Coastal Quanzhou Hokkien dialects (<span lang="zh">泉州海口腔</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">Choân-chiu Hái-kháu khiuⁿ</span></i>)
<ul><li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coastal_Nan%27an_dialect&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Coastal Nan&#39;an dialect (page does not exist)">Coastal Nan'an dialect</a> (<span lang="zh">海南安腔</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">Hái Lâm-oaⁿ khiuⁿ</span></i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hui%27an_dialect" title="Hui&#39;an dialect">Hui'an dialect</a> (<span lang="zh">惠安腔</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">Hūi-oaⁿ khiuⁿ</span></i>)
<ul><li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Toubei_dialect&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Toubei dialect (page does not exist)">Toubei dialect</a> (in <a href="/wiki/Quangang" class="mw-redirect" title="Quangang">Quangang</a>) (<span lang="zh">頭北話</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">Thâu-pak-ōe</span></i>) (Transitionary with <a href="/wiki/Pu-Xian_Min" class="mw-redirect" title="Pu-Xian Min">Pu-Xian Min</a>)</li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jinjiang,_Fujian#Language" class="mw-redirect" title="Jinjiang, Fujian">Jinjiang dialect</a> (<span lang="zh">晋江腔</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">Chìn-kang khiuⁿ</span></i>)</li>
<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Luojiang_dialect&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Luojiang dialect (page does not exist)">Luojiang dialect</a> (<span lang="zh">洛江腔</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">Lo̍k-kang khiuⁿ</span></i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Quanzhou_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Quanzhou dialect">Quanzhou City Proper dialect</a> (<span lang="zh">泉州市腔</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">Choân-chiu-chhǐ khiuⁿ</span></i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Philippine_Hokkien" title="Philippine Hokkien">Philippine Hokkien</a> (<span lang="zh">咱人話</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">Lán-nâng-ōe / Nán-nâng-ōe / Lán-lâng-ōe</span></i>)</li></ul></li>
<li>Inland Quanzhou Hokkien dialects (<span lang="zh">泉州府城腔</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">Choân-chiu Hú-siâⁿ khiuⁿ</span></i>)
<ul><li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anxi_dialect&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Anxi dialect (page does not exist)">Anxi dialect</a> (<span lang="zh">安溪腔</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">An-khoe khiuⁿ</span></i>) (Transitionary with <a href="/wiki/Zhangzhou_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Zhangzhou dialect">Zhangzhou dialect</a>)</li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Amoy_dialect" title="Amoy dialect">Amoy-Tong'an Hokkien dialects</a> (<span lang="zh">廈門同安腔</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">Ē-mn̂g Tâng-oaⁿ khiuⁿ</span></i>)
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Amoy_dialect" title="Amoy dialect">Amoy dialect</a> (<span lang="zh">廈門腔</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">Ē-mn̂g khiuⁿ</span></i>)</li>

<li>Riau Hokkien (in <a href="/wiki/Riau_Province" class="mw-redirect" title="Riau Province">Riau Province</a>, <a href="/wiki/Riau_islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Riau islands">Riau islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jambi" title="Jambi">Jambi</a>)</li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Zhangzhou_dialects" title="Zhangzhou dialects">Zhangzhou Hokkien dialects</a> (<span lang="zh">漳州腔</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">Chiang-chiu khioⁿ</span></i>):
<ul><li>Northern Zhangzhou Hokkien dialects
