<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Culture"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485"><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">November 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Quanzhou" title="Quanzhou">Quanzhou</a> was historically the cultural center for Hokkien, as various traditional Hokkien cultural customs such as <a href="/wiki/Nanguan_music" title="Nanguan music">Nanguan music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beiguan_music" title="Beiguan music">Beiguan music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glove_puppetry" title="Glove puppetry">glove puppetry</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Kaoka_opera" class="mw-redirect" title="Kaoka opera">kaoka</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lewan_opera" class="mw-redirect" title="Lewan opera">lewan</a> genres of <a href="/wiki/Hokkien_culture#Performing_arts" title="Hokkien culture">Hokkien opera</a> originated from Quanzhou. This was mainly due to the fact that Quanzhou had become an important trading and commercial port since the Tang dynasty and had prospered into an important city. After the <a href="/wiki/Opium_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Opium War">Opium War</a> in 1842, Xiamen became one of the major <a href="/wiki/Treaty_port" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty port">treaty ports</a> to be opened for trade with the outside world. From the mid-19th century onwards, Xiamen slowly developed to become the political and economical center of the Hokkien-speaking region in China. This caused the Amoy dialect to gradually replace the position of dialects from Quanzhou and <a href="/wiki/Zhangzhou" title="Zhangzhou">Zhangzhou</a>. From the mid-19th century until the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> western diplomats usually learned Amoy as the preferred dialect if they were to communicate with the Hokkien-speaking populace in China or Southeast Asia. In the 1940s and 1950s, Taiwan<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (November 2016)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> also tended towards the Amoy dialect.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Retreat_of_the_Republic_of_China_to_Taiwan" class="mw-redirect" title="Retreat of the Republic of China to Taiwan">retreat of the Republic of China to Taiwan</a> in 1949 drove party leaders to seek to both culturally and politically assimilate the islanders. As a result, laws were passed throughout the 1950s to suppress Hokkien and other languages in favor of Mandarin. By 1956, speaking Hokkien in ROC schools or military bases was illegal. However, popular outcry from both older islander communities and more recent Mainlander immigrants prompted a general wave of education reform, during which these and other education restrictions were lifted. The general goal of assimilation remained, with Amoy Hokkien seen as less 'native', and therefore preferred.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, from the 1980s onwards, the development of <a href="/wiki/Hokkien_pop" title="Hokkien pop">Taiwanese Min Nan</a> pop music and media industry in Taiwan caused the <a href="/wiki/Hokkien_culture" title="Hokkien culture">Hokkien cultural</a> hub to shift from Xiamen to Taiwan.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The flourishing <a href="/wiki/Hokkien_entertainment_media" title="Hokkien entertainment media">Taiwanese Min Nan entertainment and media industry</a> from Taiwan in the 1990s and early 21st century led Taiwan to emerge as the new significant cultural hub for Hokkien.
</p><p>In the 1990s, marked by the liberalization of language development and <a href="/wiki/Mother_tongue" class="mw-redirect" title="Mother tongue">mother tongue</a> movement in Taiwan, Taiwanese Hokkien had developed quickly. In 1993, Taiwan became the first region in the world to implement the teaching of Taiwanese Hokkien in Taiwanese schools. In 2001, the local Taiwanese language program was further extended to all schools in Taiwan, and Taiwanese Hokkien became one of the compulsory local Taiwanese languages to be learned in schools.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mother_tongue" class="mw-redirect" title="Mother tongue">mother tongue</a> movement in Taiwan even influenced Xiamen (Amoy) to the point that in 2010, Xiamen also began to implement the teaching of Hokkien dialect in its schools.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In 2007, the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Education_(Republic_of_China)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ministry of Education (Republic of China)">Ministry of Education</a> in Taiwan also completed the standardization of Chinese characters used for writing Hokkien and developed <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_Romanization_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Taiwanese Romanization System">Tai-lo</a> as the standard Hokkien pronunciation and romanization guide. A number of universities in Taiwan also offer Taiwanese degree courses for training Hokkien-fluent talents to work for the Hokkien media industry and education. Taiwan also has its own Hokkien literary and cultural circles whereby Hokkien poets and writers compose poetry or literature in Hokkien.
</p><p>Thus, by the 21st century, Taiwan had become one of the most significant Hokkien cultural hubs of the world. The historical changes and development in Taiwan had led <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_Hokkien" title="Taiwanese Hokkien">Taiwanese Hokkien</a> to become the most influential pole of the Hokkien dialect after the mid-20th century. Today, the Taiwanese prestige dialect (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">台語優勢腔／通行腔</span></span>) is heard on Taiwanese media.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_systems">Writing systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Writing systems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Written_Hokkien" title="Written Hokkien">Written Hokkien</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_Hokkien_writing_systems" title="Comparison of Hokkien writing systems">Comparison of Hokkien writing systems</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chinese_script">Chinese script</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Chinese script"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hokkien dialects are typically written using <a href="/wiki/Chinese_character" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese character">Chinese characters</a> (<span lang="zh">漢字</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">Hàn-jī</span></i>. However, the written script was and remains adapted to the literary form, which is based on <a href="/wiki/Classical_Chinese" title="Classical Chinese">classical Chinese</a>, not the vernacular and spoken form. Furthermore, the character inventory used for Mandarin (standard written Chinese) does not correspond to Hokkien words, and there are a large number of informal characters (<span lang="zh">替字</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">thè-jī, thòe-jī</span></i>; &#39;substitute characters&#39; which are unique to Hokkien, as is the case with <a href="/wiki/Written_Cantonese" title="Written Cantonese">written Cantonese</a>. For instance, about 20 to 25% of Taiwanese morphemes lack an appropriate or standard Chinese character.<sup id="cite_ref-mothertongue_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mothertongue-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While most Hokkien morphemes have standard designated characters, they are not always etymological or phono-semantic. Similar-sounding, similar-meaning or rare characters are commonly borrowed or substituted to represent a particular morpheme. Examples include "beautiful" (<span lang="zh">美</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">bí</span></i> is the literary form), whose vernacular morpheme <i><span lang="zh-Latn">suí</span></i> is represented by characters like <span lang="zh">媠</span> (an obsolete character), <span lang="zh">婎</span> (a vernacular reading of this character) and even <span lang="zh">水</span> (transliteration of the sound <i><span lang="nan-Latn">suí</span></i>), or (<span lang="zh">高</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">ko</span></i> is the literary form), whose morpheme <i><span lang="nan-Latn">kôan</span></i> is <span lang="zh">懸</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Common grammatical particles are not exempt; the negation particle <i><span lang="nan-Latn">m̄</span></i> is variously represented by <span lang="zh">毋</span>, <span lang="zh">呣</span> or <span lang="zh">唔</span>, among others. In other cases, characters are invented to represent a particular morpheme (a common example is the character <span lang="zh"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%AA%9C%B6" class="extiw" title="wikt:𪜶">𪜶</a></span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">in</span></i>, which represents the personal pronoun 'they'). In addition, some characters have multiple and unrelated pronunciations, adapted to represent Hokkien words. For example, the Hokkien word <i><span lang="nan-Latn">bah</span></i>; &#39;meat&#39;) has been reduced to the character <span lang="zh">肉</span>, which have etymologically unrelated colloquial and literary readings <i><span lang="nan-Latn">he̍k</span></i> and <i><span lang="nan-Latn">jio̍k</span></i>, respectively).<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Another case is the word <i><span lang="nan-Latn">chia̍h</span></i>; &#39;to eat&#39;, which is often transcribed in Taiwanese newspapers and media as <span lang="zh">呷</span> (a Mandarin transliteration , to approximate the Hokkien term), even though its recommended character in dictionaries is <span lang="zh">食</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Moreover, unlike Cantonese, Hokkien does not have a universally accepted standardized character set. Thus, there is some variation in the characters used to express certain words and characters can be ambiguous in meaning. In 2007, the Ministry of Education of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of China">Republic of China</a> formulated and released a standard character set to overcome these difficulties.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> These standard Chinese characters for writing <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_Hokkien" title="Taiwanese Hokkien">Taiwanese Hokkien</a> are now taught in schools in Taiwan.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_script">Latin script</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Latin script"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hokkien, especially Taiwanese Hokkien, is sometimes written in the <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a> using one of several alphabets. Of these the most popular is <a href="/wiki/Pe%CC%8Dh-%C5%8De-j%C4%AB" title="Pe̍h-ōe-jī">POJ</a>, developed first by <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian">Presbyterian</a> <a href="/wiki/Missionaries" class="mw-redirect" title="Missionaries">missionaries</a> in China and later by the indigenous <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_in_Taiwan" title="Presbyterian Church in Taiwan">Presbyterian Church in Taiwan</a>. Use of this script and orthography has been actively promoted since the late 19th century. The use of a mixed script of Han characters and Latin letters is also seen, though remains uncommon. Other Latin-based alphabets also exist.
</p><p>Min Nan texts, all Hokkien, can be dated back to the 16th century. One example is the <i><a href="/wiki/Doctrina_Christiana" title="Doctrina Christiana">Doctrina Christiana</a> en letra y lengua china</i>, presumably written around 1593 by the Spanish <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican</a> friars in the Philippines. Another is a <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> script of a play called <i><a href="/wiki/Tale_of_the_Lychee_Mirror" title="Tale of the Lychee Mirror">Tale of the Lychee Mirror</a></i> (1566), supposedly the earliest Southern Min colloquial text, although it is written in <a href="/wiki/Teochew_dialect" title="Teochew dialect">Teochew dialect</a>.
</p><p>Taiwan has developed a Latin alphabet for Taiwanese Hokkien, derived from POJ, known as Tai-lo. Since 2006, it has been officially promoted by Taiwan's Ministry of Education and taught in Taiwanese schools. <a href="/wiki/Xiamen_University" title="Xiamen University">Xiamen University</a> has also developed an alphabet based on Pinyin called <a href="/wiki/Bb%C3%A1nl%C3%A1m_p%C3%ACngy%C4%ABm" title="Bbánlám pìngyīm">Bbánlám pìngyīm</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Computing">Computing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Computing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:%E4%BA%BB%E5%9B%A0_(in).png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/%E4%BA%BB%E5%9B%A0_%28in%29.png/220px-%E4%BA%BB%E5%9B%A0_%28in%29.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/%E4%BA%BB%E5%9B%A0_%28in%29.png/330px-%E4%BA%BB%E5%9B%A0_%28in%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/%E4%BA%BB%E5%9B%A0_%28in%29.png/440px-%E4%BA%BB%E5%9B%A0_%28in%29.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1000" /></a><figcaption>The character for the third-person pronoun 'they' in some Hokkien dialects, <span lang="zh">𪜶</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">in</span></i>, is now supported by the Unicode Standard, at U+2A736.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Hokkien is registered as "Southern Min" per RFC 3066 as <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.iana.org/assignments/lang-tags/zh-min-nan">zh-min-nan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When writing Hokkien in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a>, some writers create 'new' characters when they consider it impossible to use directly or borrow existing ones; this corresponds to similar practices in character usage in <a href="/wiki/Written_Cantonese" title="Written Cantonese">Cantonese</a>, Vietnamese <a href="/wiki/Ch%E1%BB%AF_N%C3%B4m" title="Chữ Nôm">chữ Nôm</a>, Korean <a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">hanja</a> and Japanese <a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">kanji</a>. Some of these are not encoded in <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a>, thus creating problems in computer processing.
</p><p>All <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> characters required by <a href="/wiki/Pe%CC%8Dh-%C5%8De-j%C4%AB" title="Pe̍h-ōe-jī">Pe̍h-ōe-jī</a> can be represented using <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> (or the corresponding ISO/IEC 10646: Universal Character Set), using precomposed or combining (diacritics) characters. Prior to June 2004, the vowel akin to but more open than <i>o</i>, written with a dot above right, was not encoded. The usual workaround was to use an (stand-alone; spaced)  <a href="/wiki/Interpunct" title="Interpunct">interpunct</a> (U+00B7, ·) or less commonly the combining character dot above (U+0307). As these are far from ideal, since 1997 proposals have been submitted to the ISO/IEC <a href="/wiki/Working_group" title="Working group">working group</a> in charge of ISO/IEC 10646—namely, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070628163947/http://anubis.dkuug.dk/JTC1/SC2/wg2/">ISO/IEC JTC1/SC2/WG2</a>—to encode a new combining character <i>dot above right</i>. This is now officially assigned to U+0358 (see documents <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.evertype.com/standards/la/taioan.html">N1593</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070311082219/http://anubis.dkuug.dk/JTC1/SC2/WG2/docs/n2507">N2507</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20060928065416/http://anubis.dkuug.dk/JTC1/SC2/WG2/docs/n2628">N2628</a>,
<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://std.dkuug.dk/jtc1/sc2/wg2/docs/n2699">N2699</a>, and <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://std.dkuug.dk/jtc1/sc2/wg2/docs/n2713">N2713</a>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_and_political_role">Cultural and political role</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Cultural and political role"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hokkien_culture" title="Hokkien culture">Hokkien culture</a></div>
<p>Hokkien (or Min Nan) can trace its roots through the Tang dynasty and also even further to the people of the <a href="/wiki/Minyue" title="Minyue">Minyue</a>, the indigenous non-Han people of modern-day Fujian.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Min Nan (Hokkien) people call themselves "<a href="/wiki/Names_of_China#Tang" title="Names of China">Tang</a> people," (<span lang="zh">唐人</span>; <i><span lang="nan-Latn">Tn̂g-lâng</span></i>) which is synonymous to "Chinese people". Because of the widespread influence of the Tang culture during the great Tang dynasty, there are today still many Min Nan pronunciations of words shared by the <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_language" title="Vietnamese language">Vietnamese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a> and Japanese languages.
</p><p>In 2002, the <a href="/wiki/Taiwan_Solidarity_Union" title="Taiwan Solidarity Union">Taiwan Solidarity Union</a>, a party with about 10% of the <a href="/wiki/Legislative_Yuan" title="Legislative Yuan">Legislative Yuan</a> seats at the time, suggested making Taiwanese a second official language.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> This proposal encountered strong opposition not only from mainland Chinese groups but also from <a href="/wiki/Hakka" class="mw-redirect" title="Hakka">Hakka</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_aboriginal" class="mw-redirect" title="Taiwanese aboriginal">Taiwanese aboriginal</a> groups who felt that it would slight their home languages. Because of these objections, support for this measure was lukewarm among moderate <a href="/wiki/Taiwan_independence" class="mw-redirect" title="Taiwan independence">Taiwan independence</a> supporters, and the proposal did not pass.
</p><p>Hokkien was finally made an official language of Taiwan in 2018 by the ruling DPP government.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Ethnologue23&#124;nan-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Ethnologue23|nan_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Ethnologue23|nan_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.ethnologue.com/23/language/nan">Hokkien</a> at <i><a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">Ethnologue</a></i> (23rd ed., 2020) <span style="position:relative; top: -2px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/Paywall" title="closed access publication – behind paywall"><img alt="Closed access icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Closed_Access_logo_transparent.svg/9px-Closed_Access_logo_transparent.svg.png" decoding="async" width="9" height="14" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Closed_Access_logo_transparent.svg/14px-Closed_Access_logo_transparent.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Closed_Access_logo_transparent.svg/18px-Closed_Access_logo_transparent.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="1000" /></a></span></span></span>

Brill. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-90-04-18493-0" title="Special:BookSources/978-90-04-18493-0"><bdi>978-90-04-18493-0</bdi></a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Language+of+the+Sangleys%3A+A+Chinese+Vernacular+in+Missionary+Sources+of+the+Seventeenth+Century&amp;rft.pub=Brill&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft.isbn=978-90-04-18493-0&amp;rft.aulast=Kl%C3%B6ter&amp;rft.aufirst=Henning&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHokkien" class="Z3988"></span> An analysis and facsimile of the <i>Arte de la Lengua Chio-chiu</i> (1620), the oldest extant grammar of Hokkien.</li></ul>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="External_links">External links</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hokkien&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: External links"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1134653256">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:#f9f9f9;display:flow-root}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><div class="side-box side-box-right plainlinks sistersitebox"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><span class="noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Wikivoyage-Logo-v3-icon.svg/40px-Wikivoyage-Logo-v3-icon.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Wikivoyage-Logo-v3-icon.svg/60px-Wikivoyage-Logo-v3-icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Wikivoyage-Logo-v3-icon.svg/80px-Wikivoyage-Logo-v3-icon.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="193" data-file-height="193" /></span></span></div>

<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite class="citation book cs1">Cobo, Juan, O.P. (1607). <i>Doctrina Christiana</i>. Manila.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Doctrina+Christiana&amp;rft.place=Manila&amp;rft.date=1607&amp;rft.aulast=Cobo&amp;rft.aufirst=Juan%2C+O.P.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHokkien" class="Z3988"></span><span class="cs1-maint citation-comment"><code class="cs1-code">{{<a href="/wiki/Template:Cite_book" title="Template:Cite book">cite book</a>}}</code>:  CS1 maint: multiple names: authors list (<a href="/wiki/Category:CS1_maint:_multiple_names:_authors_list" title="Category:CS1 maint: multiple names: authors list">link</a>)</span> Hokkien translation of the <i><a href="/wiki/Doctrina_Christiana" title="Doctrina Christiana">Doctrina Christiana</a></i>:

Manila.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Arte+de+la+Lengua+Chio-chiu&amp;rft.place=Manila&amp;rft.date=1620&amp;rft.aulast=Man%C3%A7ano&amp;rft.aufirst=Melchior&amp;rft.au=Feyjo%C3%B3%2C+Raymundo&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fbipadi.ub.edu%2Fcdm%2Fref%2Fcollection%2Fmanuscrits%2Fid%2F29744&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHokkien" class="Z3988"></span> A manual for learning Hokkien written by a Spanish missionary in the Philippines.</li>

<dl><dd>(The voyager clip says: <span title="Min Nan Chinese-language text"><i lang="nan">Thài-khong pêng-iú, lín-hó. Lín chia̍h-pá--bē?  Ū-êng, to̍h lâi gún chia chē--ô͘!</i></span> 太空朋友，恁好。恁食飽未？有閒著來阮遮坐哦!)</dd></dl></li></ul>
